On vehicles like the Tesla Model 3 and Model Y, the charge port is located above the rear wheel on the driver's side. On other EVs, it might be above the front wheel, or on the other side of the vehicle. This means non-Tesla drivers may have to play with the positioning of their vehicles in order to reach the Supercharger cord, blocking other charging spots.

To be CLEAR, the solution is NOT to extend the length of the cable. To do so would require an increase in the diameter of the wire inside the cable to maintain current charging current (AKA charging speed). This reduces cable flexibility significantly, making it much more difficult to maneuver the cable into ALL charging ports. The solution is to have vehicle manufacturers that HAVE NOT invested in the supercharger network to change THEIR specs. As in, move the location of their charging ports. You got to pay to play, folks.

I can't believe some short-sighted people blame the short Supercharger cable, it was never designed to charge vehicles without the charge port in the standardized location for Supercharging. Ford and Rivian just wanted to be different from Tesla and obviously they never planned to use Superchargers. It's not Tesla's job to accommodate their wonky charge port location. There is a solution though and it's a no-brainer: Rivian, Ford and the rest will do what they should have done to begin with - put the charge port on the driver's side rear. That way the driver has easy access to it and it's not necessary to back out into traffic when done charging. Safe and convenient. Everyone will be happy.  Ironically, Tesla's NACS port design has been the defacto North American standard all along. There has always been more EV's, continent wide, with a NACS charging port on the driver's side rear end than EV's with CCS1 ports anywhere. It's just that legacy auto and the rest of the industry wanted to isolate Tesla by being different. But Tesla makes and sells more EV's in N. America than every other EV manufacturer combined! Tesla deserves more credit instead of all the fake innuendo sent their way. Besides, Tesla has the best value, by far, in EV's - they are bringing EV prices of quality EV's down to the point where regular blue-collar workers can afford them.

This is an excellent example of how every other manufacturer of E v's has missed an industry standard that's already been established. Tesla was 1st. Tesla is best and Tesla's the only one who built their own charging network imagine that.   Long ago ice vehicles established a logical standard of putting the refueling port on the back driver's side corner which makes sense late at night why would you want to walk around the other side of your car?  Complying with that same standard Tesla put their charging port on the back driver's side corner the most logical spot on the vehicle. Additionally gasoline companies came up with the idea that all vehicles should refuel with the same nozzle,l so all gas pumps have a standardized refueling nozzle.  So these 2 facts confirm that every other EV maker got it wrong like it or not Tesla has the largest established recharging network so you should use their charge port and their charge port location.  Remember folks you're not just buying an EV you're buying a charging network as well once again Tesla's leaps and bounds ahead of everybody else.

Superchargers have been open to all EV's since last summer in Europe, where I live. Charge port location on the cars can be an issue, but we tend to drive much less massive cars than people in NA, so you won't see those long-nosed F-150's here. As for queues: just under a year into the experiment, drivers from other brands aren't really flooding Superchargers so far. The big holiday migration to the south of Europe gave rise to charging queues at Ionity stations (the European equivalent of Electrify America) and other DC fast chargers, but only very rarely at Superchargers.

As an engineer a few words of caution. If your charge port is poorly located for super charger usage - the cable does not stretch, don‚Äôt try to stretch it. - it only bends a few times at 90deg - if its realty tight, it will damage the NACS connector - if its realty tight, it will damage the NACS to CCS connector - if its realty tight, it will damage the CCS connector in your EV, your fault - you could cause a fire As a Tesla owner you could lose my good will.  Enjoy the super charger experience.

We're getting the V4 superchargers now in Europe. Note that Tesla's also had CCS for ages here now, the tesla connector itself in theory is less capable than CCS here in general, due to the "combo" part here being wired for three-phase, which fits the European grid better (our houses have 3x25A typically, and as such a >40A single phase 240V charger is very pricey to fit, so the de-facto standard is 3x16A for a car, which makes loadbalancing easy to go alongside with it, the tesla plug we had for a short while could only charge 1-phase which meant it was a challenge to charge at home for the first model S owners, which also since have been retrofitted).  Note that trucks are pretty much a 'non thing' here, so rivians/f150's are a non-issue, and some Audi's sometimes even can charge on both sides (after all, electricity is 'easy' compared to fuel). Lot of in-car nav's that use preconditioning (like Hyundai/Kia) "do" preconditoning for Tesla as well if a SuC is set as destination/stop. So on that side, it's pretty neat.  Elephant in the room here though is the V3 charger. While V2 is fine, and I'll try a V4 soon as well, on my car (an Ioniq 5) a V3 SuC doesn't work. Why? Tesla in its infinite wisdom decided to have the lousiest negotiation protocol ever, and V3 can deliver more than 400V. Like. 500V. The Ioniq can take 800V, 400V, and stuff in between, but honestly, the negotiation between the car and the charger is so dodgy, it just charges at 50kW and eventually just stops. Tesla's not putting a lot of priority in fixing it (unlike Fastned, the 'easy and plug in' network here that always works, it has plug & charge for all brands other than some VW's because VW forgot to properly encode mac adresses).  V4 that should not be an issue anymore though, as V4 does up to 900V. V4 SuC's also come with longer cables, making cars like mine (charge port right back) easy to fit next to left-back chargers. Honestly, from a cost perspective, I simply hope that cars will have more charge ports as a standard soon, >IF< you DC charge often, that's such a huge conveinience issue.
Damn can the engineers just improve the details by actually more testing?

As a Tesla owner welcome to the Super charger network. As an engineer a few words of caution. If your charge port is poorly located for super charger usage - the cable does not stretch, don‚Äôt try to stretch it. - it only bends a few times at 90deg - if its realty tight, it will damage the NACS connector - if its realty tight, it will damage the NACS to CCS connector - if its realty tight, it will damage the CCS connector in your EV, your fault - you could cause a fire As a Tesla owner you could lose my good will.  Enjoy the super charger experience.

Short Cable length is not about the cost alone. It's more related to Power wastage as well. As you increase the cable length, you end up increasing the resistance, thereby wasting power in the form of heat. Whatmore, you lose Voltage at the receiving end too which might result in your car not even charge

@mkbhd the Tesla app "defaulted to Africa just now" because that is what is called Null Island, also known as 0, 0 in coordinates. It is where the prime meridian and the equator intersect, and from a software engineer's perspective, this would be the default place you would point to when a device's location is not yet available.

It seems to me the problem is still on Tesla's side.  The problem is similar to that of a Mac versus a PC or even an iPhone and a Android. Android has the largest market share in the smart phone market and they used typical micro USB's or even the USB-C. Apple on the other hand wanted to use the lightning adapter. The problem was, the USB and USB-C were faster, charged better and were compatible with everything, with the Apple could optimize its ecosystem around their connector.  But while the Lightning had shortcomings, it also made all those Apple spin off products exclusive to their ecosystem. Well, Apple was eventually forced to come over for consumers sake.  This is what Tesla will need to do. The Tesla connector is already at its peak. It cannot handle the higher voltages which Porsche and Hyundai use, and even some other automakers are eyeing for the future. They will need longer cords, and to use the CCS standard which apparently can handle over a thousand KWH transfer rate. Even the Tesla truck uses CCS.  If they made this change, Porsche, Ford, and other car companies can then just download a plug in that would allow them to take advantage of the plug and play of Tesla super chargers as they already can communicate with Electrify America. Its actually a decent business case.

The bigger issue are the materials to make batteries.  Nearly 50% of world cobalt reserves are in the Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC), 58% of lithium reserves are in Chile, 80% of natural graphite reserves are in China, Brazil and Turkey, while 75% of manganese reserves are in Australia, Brazil, South Africa and Ukraine.  The fight (e.g., political influence from the US and China in particular) for these materials will increase thereby substantially increasing the prices of EVs.

$0.49 per kilowatt hour without membership, $0.39  with  a $12/month membership. Note that Tesla owners get the $0.39 rate at that station, no membership required. In Colorado there is currently no non-Tesla charging capability, and the rates range from $0.20/kwh to $0.34/kwh. To better serve their Tesla owners, Tesla may increase the charging fee for non-Tesla vehicles at locations that become congested, encouraging them to only use the chargers for emergencies. Moreover, Tesla announced during its investor day on March 1, 2023, that new stations would have longer cables to accommodate non-Tesla vehicles.

It's interesting to compare the cost of charge in this video to gasoline alternative. The truck shows 88 miles at 30% and the increase to 85% charge is stated to cost $30. this works out to about 3 miles per percent charge or 165 miles of range for the $30 (5.5 miles per dollar). At the time of this post gas according to google maps is around $3.50/gallon in Brewster NY which means a similar vehicle in the same conditions would need to get roughly 20 mpg (5.5 miles/$ x 3.5 $/gallon = 19.25 miles/gallon) to match fuel cost. I do want to note that this is far from an optimal comparison scenario for the electric car because of weather conditions, greater cost of electricity for a fast charger vs home charging, and it ignores the greater maintenance costs of internal combustion vehicles. Also there's a lot of extrapolating going on.  Still, it's a little funny how the numbers came out because a 2022 3.3L EcoBoost 4x4 F150 is rated 20mpg combined.

Random charger facts: an average U.S. home has a 200-amp service panel and provides up to 240 volts current. Kilowatts are volts x amps / 1000. So, the most power a home charger could provide is only 48 kw, less than one-fifth of what a single Tesla 250 kw "Supercharger" kiosk provides!

At 1:30, the reason the map opens on Africa is because it's defaulting to the 0¬∞0¬∞ coordinates, which is in the Gulf of Guinea off the coast of West Africa. It does that because the geolocation is too slow and it's not coded well enough to pick a sane default location like your home address or last known location. It's an easy fix for Tesla software engineers, but I'd guess that the PM isn't aware of it because they don't test that feature because they all drive Teslas.

Simple...They need to lengthen the cables! You can't use an extension cable, because the charge cables are actively cooled. Some are internally air cooled, while the high power chargers, (usually 250 kW or higher) are liquid cooled. An extension core would either need to be impossibly heavy, or, you would have to settle for a maximum charge rate of ~50 kW. That would take more of your time, as well as tying up a charge station for 3X longer, to reach the desired state of charge.

The problem that's not being discussed is the cost comparison between owning a Tesla and a traditional hybrid. As a Tesla owner myself, I've noticed that it's actually more expensive to own a Tesla than a hybrid like the Rav4. The Rav4 hybrid can get 35mpg at 80mph, and even up to 45mpg in some cases, for around $3.50 per gallon (as of 3/1/22). This means that to travel 100 miles, you would only need 2.5 gallons of gas, which would cost you $8.75.  However, with my Model Y Performance, even driving at 75mph, I typically get around 320-340 whr/mile. This means that I would need around 32kw on the low end to travel 100 miles, which is nearly half of the battery capacity at a super low rate of 32 cents per KW. It's worth noting that in a non-Tesla, you could see rates as high as 50 cents per KW. So, if you do the math, 32kw x $0.32 = $10.24. In other words, even the most expensive gas on a hybrid is still cheaper than the cheapest 32 cents per KW on a Model Y.  If you compare the Model 3 Long Range to a Camry or Corolla hybrid that gets around 50mpg, you'll find that electric cars not only cost more upfront but also cost more to charge.

This adapter Tesla built in works surprisingly well. It's a really clever solution really.
Just a thing to keep in mind for folks new to EVs. This cable adapter only works with vehicles that support DCFC. The connector is for CCS (Combined Charging Systems); the circle with 3 holes and oval on bottom with 2. Older vehicles like the Chevrolet Volt, for example, only support AC charging or just the top circle (J1172). The Tesla converter won't fit with these. Most newer EVs will support DC fast charging, so you can take advantage of the Tesla chargers if you'd like.
